The 'Zanata fi al-Jabal' Dispute: Context and Hadd in Qadhf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ambiguous word meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ambiguous words in qadhf and apply the majority view that context (qarina) determines the intended meaning.

Core Idea
Context overrides literal meaning when a word is used in anger—the angry tone itself is a proof of intent.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific phrase: 'Zanata fi al-jabal'. The speaker claims he meant 'you climbed the mountain', but the context of anger suggests an accusation of fornication. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that hadd is applied, while Muhammad al-Shaybani argues for literal meaning. The dispute reveals how Hanafi usul balances linguistic fact with contextual evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify what was said ('Zanata fi al-jabal') and what the speaker claimed he meant ('I meant climbing the mountain').
The phrase is ambiguous: the word with hamzah can mean climbing, without hamzah means fornication.
2
Identify the two positions
State that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule hadd is applied, while Muhammad al-Shaybani rules it is not.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine Muhammad's proof
Analyze Muhammad's argument: the word with hamzah is literally for climbing, supported by an Arab woman's usage and the mention of 'mountain'.
He cites a poetic example: 'ascend to goodness as climbing (zina'an) in the mountain'.
4
Examine the proof of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Understand their counter: the word is used for fornication even with hamzah due to linguistic interchange, and the angry context specifies the intended meaning.
They analogize to explicit accusations like 'ya zani'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical difference: under Muhammad's view the speaker is not punished; under the majority view he is. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The dispute hinges on the weight given to linguistic literalness vs. contextual indication (qarina).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ لِغَيْرِهِ زَنَاتَ فِي الْجَبَلِ وَقَالَ عَنَيْتُ صُعُودَ الْجَبَلِ حُدَّ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيْفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ لَا يُحَدُّ لِأَنَّ الْمَهْمُوزَ مِنْهُ لِلصُّعُودِ حَقِيقَةٌ وَالْمُلَيَّنُ لِلْفَاحِشَةِ مَجَازٌ قَالَتِ امْرَأَةٌ مِنَ الْعَرَبِ ع وَارْقَ إِلَى الْخَيْرَاتِ زِنَاءً فِي الْجَبَلِ وَذِكْرُ الْجَبَلِ يُقَرِّرُهُ مُرَادًا وَلَهُمَا إِنَّهُ يُسْتَعْمَلُ فِي الْفَاحِشَةِ مَهْمُوزًا أَيْضًا لِأَنَّ مِنَ الْعَرَبِ مَنْ يَعْمُرُ الْمُلَيَّنَ كَمَا يُلَيَّنُ الْمَهْمُوزُ وَحَالَةُ الْغَضَبِ وَالسَّبَابِ تُعَيِّنُ الْفَاحِشَةَ مُرَادًا بِمَنْزِلَةِ مَا إِذَا قَالَ يَا زَانِي أَوْ قَالَ زَنَيْتَ وَذِكْرُ الْجَبَلِ إِنَّمَا يُعَيِّنُ الصُّعُودَ مُرَادًا إِذَا كَانَ مَقْرُونًا بِكَلِمَةِ عَلَى إِذْ هُوَ الْمُسْتَعْمَلُ فِيهِ وَلَوْ قَالَ زَنَيْتَ عَلَى الْجَبَل قِيلَ لَا يُحَدُّ لِمَا قَلْنَا وَقِيلَ يُحَدُّ لِلْمَعْنَى الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ
And whoever says to another 'Zanata fi al-jabal' and says 'I meant climbing the mountain' — he is punished with hadd, and this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: He is not punished, because the word with hamzah (zana'a) is literally for climbing, and the one with no hamzah (zana) is figuratively for immorality. An Arab woman said: 'Ascend to goodness as climbing (zina'an) in the mountain.' Mentioning the mountain confirms that meaning as intended. For them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): It is used for immorality even with hamzah, because some Arabs treat the lightened word like the hamzated one, and the state of anger and cursing determines immorality as intended, just as if he said 'O fornicator' or 'you fornicated'. Mentioning the mountain only determines climbing as intended when accompanied by the word 'ala', because that is its usual usage. If he said 'Zanayta 'ala al-jabal', it is said he is not punished for the reason we stated, and it is said he is punished for the meaning we mentioned.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the word 'zanata' (with or without hamzah) when used in a phrase that can mean either 'you climbed/ascended' or 'you committed fornication'. The ruling: If someone says 'Zanata fi al-jabal' (literally 'you acted in the mountain'), and claims he meant 'you climbed the mountain', Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that he is still subjected to hadd (the punishment for slander) because in a context of anger and cursing, the word is understood to refer to fornication. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, arguing that the word with hamzah (zana'a) literally means climbing, while the one without hamzah (zana) is used figuratively for fornication. He supports his view with an example from an Arab woman who used 'zina'an' to mean climbing. Moreover, mentioning 'al-jabal' reinforces the climbing meaning. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf counter that some Arabs interchange the hamzated and lightened forms, so 'zanata' can also mean 'you fornicated'. Additionally, the angry context specifies the intended meaning as fornication. If the phrase is 'Zanayta 'ala al-jabal' instead of 'fi al-jabal', there is a difference of opinion: some say no hadd because 'ala' clarifies climbing, others say hadd due to context of anger.

Key Terms
مهموز (mahmūz): A word with a hamzah; in this context, 'zanata' (with hamzah) meaning climbing.
ملين (mulayyan): A word without hamzah (lightened); 'zana' meaning fornication.
قارنة (qārina): Contextual evidence that indicates intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if someone angrily says to another 'Zanata fi al-jabal' and later claims he meant climbing, what is the ruling?
A) He is not punished because the literal meaning is climbing.
B) He is punished with hadd because context indicates fornication.
C) He is only punished if he uses the word with hamzah.
D) He is punished only if he says 'Zanayta ala al-jabal'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, contextual evidence (qarina) can override literal meaning when determining criminal intent in qadhf cases.

Ruling on Mutual Slander between Two Unrelated Individuals
Ruling on reciprocal qadhf between strangers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two unrelated persons accuse each other of zina and explain the linguistic reasoning based on the word 'بل'.

Core Idea
When two people slander each other, both are equally liable for hadd.

In this lesson, we examine a scenario where two unrelated individuals exchange accusations of zina. The Hanafi school rules that both are subject to hadd al-qadhf because the response 'بل' (rather) effectively repeats the accusation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Each person called the other 'zani'. Both have committed qadhf (slander of zina).
2
Analyze the response
The second person says 'لا بل أنت' (No, rather you). The word 'بل' is a conjunction used to correct a mistake, so it reaffirms the accusation back to the first.
In Arabic grammar, 'بل' indicates retraction and correction, making the second statement a repetition of the same accusation.
3
Apply the ruling
Since both have slandered each other, each is liable for hadd al-qadhf (80 lashes). The ruling is symmetric.
4
Conclude
Both are punished with hadd; no exception applies because they are unrelated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ لِآخَرَ يَا زَانِي فَقَالَ لَا بَلْ أَنْتَ فَإِنَّهُمَا يُحَدَّانِ لِأَنْ مَعْنَاهُ لَا بَلْ أَنْتَ زَانٍ إِذْ هِيَ كَلِمَةُ عَطْفٍ يُسْتَدْرَكُ بِهَا الْغَلَطُ فَيَصِيرُ الْخَيْرُ الْمَذْكُورُ فِي الْأَوَّلِ مَذْكُورًا فِي الثَّانِي وَمَنْ قَالَ لِامْرَأَتِهِ يَا زَانِيَةً فَقَالَتْ لَا بَلْ أَنْتَ حُدَّتِ الْمَرْأَةُ وَلَا لِعَانَ لِأَنَّهُمَا قَاذِفَانِ وَقَذْفُهُ يُوجِبُ اللِّعَانَ وَقَذْفُهَا الْحَدَّ وَفِي الْبِدَايَةِ بِالْحَدِّ إِبْطَالُ اللِّعَانِ لَا الْمَحْدُودَ فِي الْقَذْفِ لَيْسَ بِأَهْلٍ لَهُ وَلَا إِنْطَالَ فِي عَكْسِهِ أَصْلًا فَيُحْتَالُ لِلدَّرْءِ إِذ اللِّعَانُ فِي مَعْنَى الْحَدِّ
And whoever says to another 'O fornicator', and he says 'No, rather you', then both are subjected to hadd, because its meaning is 'No, rather you are a fornicator', since it is a word of conjunction by which the error is retracted, so the news mentioned in the first becomes mentioned in the second. And if a man says to his wife 'O fornicatress', and she says 'No, rather you', the woman is subjected to hadd, and there is no li'an, because both are slanderers. His slander necessitates li'an, and her slander necessitates hadd. In the commencement with hadd, there is nullification of li'an, because the one subjected to hadd in qadhf is not eligible for it, and there is no nullification in its reverse at all. So a stratagem is employed to prevent it, since li'an is in the meaning of hadd.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First, if two people accuse each other of zina (e.g., person A says 'O fornicator' to B, and B responds 'No, rather you'), both are liable for hadd al-qadhf. The reason is that the response 'بل' (bal) is a conjunction that corrects a previous error, thus the accusation in the first statement is effectively repeated in the second. So each has slandered the other. Second, if the accuser is a husband accusing his wife, and she responds with a counter-accusation, the legal consequences differ: the wife receives hadd, but no li'an (mutual imprecation) occurs between them. This is because the husband's slander normally triggers li'an (if he accuses her of zina and they are married), but the wife's slander triggers hadd. Since li'an is like hadd in terms of punishment (it eliminates the hadd for the husband and ends the marriage), and the wife is subjected to hadd first, she becomes ineligible for li'an. So the marriage does not end via li'an; only the wife is punished. This is a pragmatic strategy (hila) to avoid li'an when it is not appropriate.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): Slander of zina against a chaste person, punishable by hadd.
بل (bal): A corrective conjunction meaning 'rather' or 'nay', used to retract and correct a statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two unrelated men exchange 'ya zani' and 'la bal anta', what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Only the first is punished
B) Only the second is punished
C) Both are punished with hadd
D) Neither is punished because the words cancel

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a counter-accusation of zina using 'بل' makes both parties equally guilty of slander.

Ruling on a Wife’s Counter-Accusation of Zina to Her Husband
Ruling on spousal reciprocal qadhf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a wife who counter-accuses her husband of zina receives hadd rather than li'an, and describe the legal stratagem involved.

Core Idea
When a wife slanders her husband, hadd is applied to her, precluding li'an.

This lesson deals with a scenario where a husband accuses his wife of zina, and she responds with a counter-accusation. The Hanafi ruling is that the wife receives hadd, and no li'an (mutual imprecation) occurs, because the wife's slander is treated like that of any outsider, and applying hadd first makes li'an impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
A husband and wife. The husband calls his wife 'zaniyya'; she responds 'la bal anta'.
2
Understand the husband's slander
Normally, a husband who accuses his wife of zina without witnesses must undergo li'an to avoid hadd. Here, he has slandered her.
Li'an involves mutual oaths and results in separation.
3
Analyze the wife's counter-slander
The wife's response is a slander of zina against her husband. As a wife, she is not a husband, so she is treated like a non-relative slanderer: her slander incurs hadd, not li'an.
4
Apply the ruling
The wife is subjected to hadd (80 lashes). The husband's li'an is nullified because the wife is already punished. No li'an occurs, so the marriage does not end via that process.
The legal maxim: 'لا ينقلب' (it does not reverse) means hadd cannot be undone.
5
Note the stratagem (hila)
Applying hadd to the wife first is a legal stratagem to prevent li'an. Since li'an is equivalent to hadd in effect, once hadd is executed, li'an is no longer possible.
This preserves the marriage and punishes the wife for her slander.

Source Text

And whoever says to another 'O fornicator', and he says 'No, rather you', then both are subjected to hadd, because its meaning is 'No, rather you are a fornicator', since it is a word of conjunction by which the error is retracted, so the news mentioned in the first becomes mentioned in the second. And if a man says to his wife 'O fornicatress', and she says 'No, rather you', the woman is subjected to hadd, and there is no li'an, because both are slanderers. His slander necessitates li'an, and her slander necessitates hadd. In the commencement with hadd, there is nullification of li'an, because the one subjected to hadd in qadhf is not eligible for it, and there is no nullification in its reverse at all. So a stratagem is employed to prevent it, since li'an is in the meaning of hadd.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First, if two people accuse each other of zina (e.g., person A says 'O fornicator' to B, and B responds 'No, rather you'), both are liable for hadd al-qadhf. The reason is that the response 'بل' (bal) is a conjunction that corrects a previous error, thus the accusation in the first statement is effectively repeated in the second. So each has slandered the other. Second, if the accuser is a husband accusing his wife, and she responds with a counter-accusation, the legal consequences differ: the wife receives hadd, but no li'an (mutual imprecation) occurs between them. So the marriage does not end via li'an; only the wife is punished. This is a pragmatic strategy (hila) to avoid li'an when it is not appropriate.

Key Terms
لعان (li'ān): Mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of zina without witnesses, resulting in separation and the wife being spared hadd.
حيلة (ḥīla): A legal stratagem; here, applying hadd to the wife first to prevent li'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

6 min
Ambiguous Accusation and the Hadd of Qadhf
Opening of Bab Hadd al-Qadhf with first ruling on ambiguous accusation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafī school imposes the hadd of qadhf for ambiguous words like 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' when spoken in anger, and distinguish the conditions under which it would not apply.

Core Idea
Context and anger determine meaning: ambiguous words can trigger hadd if they imply zina in customary usage.

This lesson examines the Hanafī approach to interpreting ambiguous accusations in the context of qadhf. The key example is the phrase 'zanaytu fi al-jabal', which could mean 'I climbed the mountain' or 'I committed zina in the mountain.' The Hanafī school, particularly Abu Hanīfa and Abū Yūsuf, holds that the context of anger and abuse determines that zina is intended, thus imposing the hadd. We will explore their proof and the conditions that affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is saying ambiguous words that could imply zina. The ruling is that the hadd of qadhf (80 lashes) is imposed if the context indicates zina.
The punishment applies to the accuser if the accusation is false and not proven.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is based on Arabic usage and customary context (urf), and the state of anger. The Hanafī school uses the principle that when words are ambiguous, the intention is discerned from customary usage and accompanying circumstances (qarā'in al-ahwāl).
The dalil is a type of qiyas khafi (subtle analogy).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does context specify meaning? The word 'zanā' can mean both climbing and zina, but the context of anger and abuse (common in quarrels) indicates that the intended meaning is the lewd act. The pronunciation with hamza instead of alif also points to zina, as some Arabs substitute hamza for alif in this word.
The phrase 'fi al-jabal' alone does not disambiguate; it is the surrounding circumstances that determine the meaning.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is a difference of opinion regarding the preposition 'alā. Some scholars hold that 'alā al-jabal clearly indicates climbing, so hadd is not imposed. Others maintain that the context of anger overrides the preposition, so hadd is imposed.
This is an internal Hanafī ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the Hanafī tarjīḥ
The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is that hadd is imposed when context indicates zina. Al-Kāsānī and al-Marghinānī prefer the view of Abu Hanīfa, which considers the state of anger as decisive.
The opposite view is considered weaker because it overlooks the contextual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حد القذف
اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم ہے۔ جیسا کہ عرب کی ایک عورت نے اپنے لڑکے کو مخاطب کر کے کہا ہے ۔ ع ۔ وارق الی الخیرات زنا فی الجبل ۔ یعنی پہاڑ پر چڑھنے کی طرح خوبیوں کی طرح چڑھ جا۔ اور یہاں پر پہاڑوں کا ذکر کرنا اس معنی کو متعین کرتا ہے۔ کہ پہاڑ پر چڑھنا ہی مراد ہے اور پہاڑ میں زنا کر نا مراد نہیں ہے اور امام ابو حنیفہ وابو یوسف رحمتہ اللہ علیہما کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ زنات (الف کے ساتھ ) کی طرح زنات (ہمزہ کے ساتھ ) بھی بدفعلی اور فاحشہ حرکت کے معنی میں بولا جاتا ہے۔ کیونکہ کچھ عرب ایسے بھی ہیں جو خفیف الف کو ہمزہ سے بدل لیتے ہیں۔ جیسے ہمزہ کو الف کی آواز سے ادا کرتے ہیں اور غصہ گالی گلوچ کی حالت اسی خراب اور فاحشہ عمل ( زناء) مراد ہونے کو متعین کرتی ہے اور اگر اس کے عوض یوں کہا یا زانی یا یوں کہا ز نائت تو بھی یہی مقصود ہوگا۔ اور جبل ( پہاڑ) کا ذکر کرتا اسی وقت چڑھائی کے معنی میں مراد ہونا متعین ہوگا جب فی الجبل کے عوض انجیل کہے۔ یعنی تم پہاڑ پر چڑھنا۔ کیونکہ یہی محاورہ مستعمل ہے اور اگر یوں کہا ز نبات على الجبل تو بعضوں نے کہا ہے کہ حد نہیں لگائی جائے گی کیونکہ حرف علی کے ساتھ ہونے سے چڑھنے ہی کے معنی متعین ہیں۔ پہاڑ پر چڑھے اور بعضوں نے کہا ہے کہ حد قذف لگائی جائے گی کیونکہ غصہ کی حالت میں ہونے سے بدکاری ہی کے معنی متعین ہیں۔ اس لئے اس کی مراد ہوگی کہ تم نے پہاڑ میں زنا کیا ہے۔
تشريح .... ومن قال لغيره زنات في الجبل وقال عتمت صعود الجبل حدو هذا عند ابي حنيفة .. الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔ ایک نے دوسرے کو کہایا زانی دوسرے نے جواب کہا لا بل انت کس کو حد جاری کی جائے گی
Chapter of the Punishment for Slanderous Accusation. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary on Hidayah volume six. For example, an Arab woman addressed her son saying: 'Ascend to goodness like climbing a mountain.' The mention of mountains here specifies the meaning of climbing, not zina in the mountain. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is that 'zanāt' (with alif) and 'zanāt' (with hamza) are both used to mean immoral and lewd action, because some Arabs replace the light alif with hamza, as they pronounce hamza with the sound of alif. The state of anger and abuse determines that the bad and lewd act (zina) is intended. And if he said instead 'yā zānī' or 'yā zāniya', the same would be intended. Mentioning 'jabal' only specifies the meaning of climbing when instead of 'fī al-jabal' he says 'injal' (i.e., you climb the mountain), because this is the common expression. And if he said 'zanaytu 'alā al-jabal', some said the hadd is not imposed because the preposition 'alā' specifies climbing, and some said the hadd of qadhf is imposed because the state of anger specifies lewdness. So the meaning would be that you committed zina in the mountain. Elaboration: And whoever said to another 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' and said 'I meant climbing the mountain' is subject to hadd; this is the view of Abu Hanifa... The meaning is clear from the translation. One person said to another 'yā zānī' and the other replied 'lā bal anta', who will be punished?
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the punishment for false accusation of zina (qadhf). The author first addresses the interpretation of ambiguous words. He gives an example: if someone says 'zanaytu fi al-jabal', the word 'zanaytu' can mean climbing (from 'zanā') or committing zina (from 'zanā' with a different root). The author explains that the context of anger or abuse determines the meaning, especially when the word is pronounced with a hamza instead of alif, as some Arabs do. He also discusses the difference between 'fi al-jabal' (in the mountain) and 'alā al-jabal' (on the mountain). The ruling depends on the speaker's intent and the circumstances. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that such an expression in anger implies zina, so the hadd of qadhf is imposed unless the speaker can prove he meant climbing. The passage then transitions to a second scenario where one person says 'yā zānī' and the other responds 'lā bal anta'.

Key Terms
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina (unlawful sexual intercourse) against a married or chaste person.
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law; here specifically the punishment for qadhf (80 lashes).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Hadd is imposed because the context of anger specifies zina.
C) Hadd is imposed only if the accuser admits he meant zina.
D) Hadd is imposed but reduced to 40 lashes.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafī school, ambiguous words implying zina when spoken in anger trigger the hadd of qadhf, because context and customary usage determine meaning.

Mutual Qadhf: Both Parties Punished
Ruling when two persons exchange the accusation of zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when two persons exchange the accusation of zina and explain why both are punished.

Core Idea
A responsive accusation using 'bal' makes both parties liable for hadd al-qadhf.

This passage presents a clear ruling from the Hanafi school on mutual qadhf. When one person accuses another of zina, and the accused responds by redirecting the accusation using the word 'bal', both become guilty of qadhf. The linguistic function of 'bal' turns the response into an independent accusation, making both accusers and both subject to the fixed punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Person A says 'yā zānī' to B; B responds 'lā bal anta'. The ruling is that both are given the hadd punishment for qadhf.
The ruling applies regardless of who initiated the accusation.
2
Understand the linguistic element
The word 'bal' is a conjunction that negates the preceding statement and affirms the same predicate (zānī) for the respondent. Thus B's response is interpreted as 'no, rather you are a zānī'.
Without 'bal', the response might not constitute a direct accusation.
3
Explain the legal consequence
Because B's statement is a counter-accusation of zina, B becomes a qādhif and is liable for hadd just like A. Both are punished with 80 lashes each.
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and is agreed upon by the schools.
4
Confirm the absence of dispute
The text does not present any dispute; the ruling is straightforward and relied upon for fatwa.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is absent; both Sahibayn agree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ لِأَخَرَيَازَانِي فَقَالَ لَا بَلْ أَنْتَ فَإِنَّهُمَا يُحَدَّانِ لِاَنْ مَعْنَاهُ لَا بَلْ أَنْتَ زَانٍ إِذْهِيَ كَلِمَةُ عَطْفٍ يُسْتَدْرَكُ بِهَا الْغَلْطُ فَيَصِيرُ الْخَيْرُ الْمَذْكُورُ فِي الْأَوَّلِ مَذْكُورًا فِي الثَّانِي
ترجمہ اگر کسی نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ یازانی ۔ تب اس نے جواب میں کہہ دیا نہیں بلکہ تم ہو تو ان دونوں کو حد قذف لگائی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ دوسرے کے کہنے کے معنی ہیں کہ نہیں بلکہ تم زانی ہو۔ کیونکہ کلمہ ، بل عطف کے لئے آتا ہے۔ جس سے پہلے جملہ کی غلطی دور کی جاتی ہے۔ اس طرح پہلے جملہ میں جو خبر مذ کو بھی وہی دوسرے جملہ میں مذکور ہو جائے گی۔
And whoever says to another 'yā zānī' and he says 'lā bal anta', then both are punished (with hadd) because the meaning is 'no, rather you are a zānī', since 'bal' is a conjunction used to correct an error, so the predicate mentioned in the first becomes mentioned in the second. Translation: If someone says to another 'yā zānī' and he replies 'no, rather you are', then both receive the hadd of qadhf, because the meaning of the second's statement is 'no, rather you are a zānī'. The word 'bal' comes for conjunction, by which the error of the first sentence is removed, so the predicate mentioned in the first becomes mentioned in the second.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: if person A says to person B 'yā zānī' (O adulterer), and person B responds 'lā bal anta' (no, rather you are), both are subject to the hadd punishment for qadhf. The reason is that B's response is interpreted as an accusation against A. The word 'bal' serves to negate the previous statement and affirm the same predicate for the respondent. Thus B has effectively accused A of zina, making both accusers and both liable for hadd. This ruling is straightforward and does not involve any proof or dispute within the text.

Key Terms
بل (bal): A conjunction that negates the preceding statement and affirms the same for the speaker, used to correct an error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only A is punished.
B) Only B is punished because he responded.
C) Both A and B are punished.
D) Neither is punished because it was a response.

Key Takeaway
When one accuses another of zina and the respondent uses 'bal' to redirect the accusation, both are liable for hadd al-qadhf.

Mutual Spousal Qadhf: Hadd Over Li'an
Ruling when wife counters husband's qadhf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when a wife responds to her husband's qadhf with a counter-accusation, and explain why hadd takes priority over li'an.

Core Idea
When both spouses accuse each other of zina, the wife's hadd is executed first, nullifying li'an.

This passage addresses a specific scenario where a husband accuses his wife of zina, and she responds by accusing him. The Hanafi school gives priority to executing the hadd punishment on the wife, which then eliminates the need for li'an. This is a legal stratagem (hīla) rooted in the principle that li'an is a form of testimony, and a person punished for qadhf loses the capacity to testify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering act
The husband says 'yā zāniya' (O adulteress) to his wife. This is qadhf, which normally requires either proof of zina or li'an.
Li'an is the mutual oath procedure between spouses when the husband accuses without witnesses.
2
Identify the wife's response
The wife says 'lā bal anta' (No, rather you are). This is a counter-accusation of zina, which is also qadhf.
The wife's accusation makes her liable to hadd for qadhf.
3
Determine the legal conflict
Both are now accusers: the husband's qadhf normally leads to li'an, while the wife's qadhf leads to hadd. The two procedures cannot both be executed simultaneously.
The question is which takes precedence.
4
Apply the rule of priority
Hadd is executed first on the wife. Once punished for qadhf, the wife loses the legal capacity to perform li'an (since li'an is a form of testimony, and a qadhf convict cannot testify). This nullifies the husband's obligation to do li'an.
The text says: 'By executing the hadd first, li'an is nullified.'
5
Understand the hīla (legal stratagem)
The ruling deliberately prioritizes hadd to avoid li'an, because li'an is considered in the meaning of hadd (a severe oath). Dropping li'an via this stratagem prevents unnecessary complications.
This is a Hanafi device to reconcile conflicting interests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ لِامْرَأَتِهِ يَا زَانِيَةً فَقَالَتْ لَا بَلْ أَنتَ حُدَّتِ الْمَرْأَةُ وَلَا لِعَانَ لِأَنَّهُمَا قَاذِفَانِ وَقَذْقُهُ يُوجِبُ اللَّعَانَ وَقَذْفُهَا الحد وَفِى الْبِدَايَةِ بِالْحَدِابْطَالُ العَانِ لاَ الْمَحْدُودَ فِى الْقَلْفِ لَيْسَ بِأَهْلِ لَهُ وَلَا إِنْطَالَ فِي عَكْسِهِ اصلا فَيُحْتَالُ لِدَرْءِ إِذ اللعان في معنى الْحَدِ
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا یا زانیہ اوراس پر بیوی نے کہ دیا نہیں بلکہ تم ہو تو عورت کو حد قذف لگائی جائے گی اور دونوں میں لعان نہیں ہوگا۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ شوہر اور بیوی دونوں ایک دوسرے پر تہمت لگانے والے ہو گئے اور شوہر کے قذف کرنے سے لعان واجب ہوتا ہے اور عورت کے قذف کرنے سے حد واجب ہوتی ہے۔ ایسی صورت میں عورت پر پہلے حد جاری کر دینے سے لعان کی صورت ختم ہو جاتی ہے کیونکہ جس پر حد قذف پہلے جاری کر دی گئی ہو وہ لعان کرنے کے قابل نہیں رہتا ہے اور پہلے لعان کرنے میں حد قذف باطل نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ اس لئے لعان کو دور کرنے کے لئے یہی حیلہ ہوگا کیونکہ لعان تو حد زنا کے معنی میں ہے۔
And whoever says to his wife 'yā zāniya' and she says 'lā bal anta', the woman is punished (with hadd) and there is no liʿān between them, because both are accusers (qādhifs). His qadhf necessitates liʿān, and her qadhf necessitates hadd. In the beginning (al-Bidāyah) it is said that by executing the hadd first, liʿān is nullified; the one punished in qadhf is not eligible for liʿān, and there is no nullification of hadd in the opposite case at all. So a hīla (legal stratagem) is adopted to drop liʿān because liʿān is in the meaning of hadd. Translation: And if someone says to his wife 'O adulteress' and she says 'no, rather you are', the woman is punished with the hadd of qadhf and there will be no liʿān between them. The reason is that both the husband and wife have become accusers of each other. The husband's qadhf makes liʿān obligatory, and the wife's qadhf makes hadd obligatory. In this situation, by executing hadd on the wife first, the possibility of liʿān is eliminated, because a person who has been punished with hadd for qadhf is no longer eligible for liʿān. And if liʿān is performed first, the hadd is not nullified. Therefore, this is the hīla to drop liʿān, because liʿān is in the meaning of a hadd.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific scenario: a husband accuses his wife of zina (yā zāniya), and the wife responds by accusing him in return (lā bal anta). The ruling is that the wife receives the hadd punishment (80 lashes), and the husband does not perform liʿān (mutual oaths). The reasoning: both are now accusers. The husband's accusation normally leads to liʿān (since he accuses his wife of zina without witnesses), while the wife's accusation leads to hadd (since she accuses him, but liʿān is not available to her because liʿān is specifically for a husband accusing his wife). When both punishments are possible, the jurists prefer to execute the hadd first, because once a person is punished for qadhf, they lose the legal capacity to perform liʿān (since liʿān is considered a form of testimony, and a person punished for qadhf is no longer trustworthy to testify). Therefore, by punishing the wife first, the liʿān is effectively dropped. This is considered a legal stratagem (hīla) to avoid liʿān in this case. The text mentions that if liʿān were done first, it would not nullify the hadd, so the preferred order is to implement hadd first.

Key Terms
لعان (liʿān): Mutual oath-taking between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of zina without witnesses, leading to separation.
حیلہ (hīla): A legal stratagem to achieve a desirable result while remaining within the framework of the law.
قذف (qadhf): Falsely accusing a chaste person of zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'yā zāniya' to his wife and she says 'lā bal anta', what happens according to the Hanafī school?
A) The husband does liʿān and the wife is not punished.
B) Both do liʿān.
C) The wife is punished with hadd and there is no liʿān.
D) The husband is punished with hadd and the wife does liʿān.

Key Takeaway
When both spouses accuse each other of zina, the Hanafi school executes the wife's hadd first, which nullifies the liʿān procedure.

Wife's Response 'Zanaytu bika' — No Hadd, No Liʿān
Ruling on ambiguous response in mutual accusation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife responds 'zanaytu bika' to her husband's accusation of zina and explain how the principle of doubt (shubha) drops both hadd and liʿān.

Core Idea
Doubt about meaning blocks both punishments: hadd and liʿān are dropped.

In the previous lesson, we examined the case where the wife responds with silence or a vague denial. Here, the text presents a more ambiguous reply: 'zanaytu bika' (I committed zina with you). Does this confession trigger punishment? The Hanafi school rules that the ambiguity in her statement creates doubt, and according to the maxim 'punishments are averted by doubts,' both hadd and liʿān are suspended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the initial accusation
The husband says 'yā zāniya' to his wife. She responds with 'zanaytu bika'. Determine the exact context: she is replying to his accusation.
2
Unpack the two possible meanings of her response
Analyze the two interpretations given in the text: she might be admitting to zina before marriage (confirming his accusation, so she should receive hadd) or she might be asserting that she only committed zina with him after marriage (i.e., she is chaste, making his accusation false and requiring liʿān from him).
The ambiguity is key.
3
Apply the principle of doubt (shubha)
Because both meanings are possible, there is shak (doubt) about what she actually intended. The Hanafi maxim 'al-ḥudūd tudra'u bi al-shubahāt' (punishments are averted by doubts) is applied.
This is a foundational principle in hudud.
4
Derive the ruling
Since doubt exists, neither hadd (for zina) nor liʿān (for qadhf) can be carried out. The ruling is that both are dropped. The text concludes فَلَا حَدَّ وَلَا لِعَانَ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَتْ زَنَيْتُ بِكَ فَلَا حَدَّوَ لَا لِعَانَ وَمَعْنَاهُ قَالَتْ بَعْدَ مَا قَالَ لَهَا يَا زَانِيَةُ لِوُقُوعِ الشَّكِ فِي كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا لأَنَّهُ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنَّهَا اَرَادَتِ الزِّنَاءَ قَبْلَ النِّكَاحِ فَيَجِبُ الْحَدُّ دُونَ اللَّعَانِ لِتَصْدِ يُقِهَا إِيَّاهُ وَإِنْعِدَامِهِ مِنْهُ وَيَحْتَمِلُهُ انَّهَا اَرَادَتْ زِنَانِي مَا كَانَ مَعَكَ بَعْدَ النِكَاح لانّى مَا مَكَنْتُ أَحَدًا غَيْرَكَ وَهُوَ الْمُرَادُ فِي مِثْلِ هَذِهِ الْحَالَةِ وَعَلَى هذَا الْإِعْتِبَارِ يَجِبُ العَانُ دُونَ الْحَةِ عَلَى الْمَرْأَةِ لِوُجُودِ الْقَدْفِ مِنْهُ وَعَدْمِهِ مِنْهَا فَجَاءَ مَا قُلْنَا ترجمہ اور اگر بیوی نے شوہر کے ( اوزانیہ کے ) جواب میں کہا کہ ہاں میں نے تمہارے ساتھ ہی تو زنا کیا ہے۔ تو حد واجب نہ ہوگی اور نہ لعان واجب ہوگا۔ یعنی شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا یا زانیہ تو اس نے اس کے جواب میں کہا میں نے تمہارے ساتھ زنا کیا ہے۔ تو صداجان میں سے کچھ بھی واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ دونوں میں سے ہر ایک میں شک پیدا ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ شاید عورت کی مراد یہ ہو کہ میں نے نکاح سے پہلے تم سے زنا کیا ہے۔ تو ایسا ۔ ہونے سے حد واجب ہوگی اور لعان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عورت نے شوہر کے قول کی تصدیق کی مگر شوہر کی طرف سے اسکی تصدیق نہیں پائی گئی اور
And if she says 'I committed zina with you', then there is no hadd and no liʿān. Its meaning: she said it after he said 'yā zāniya' to her, due to doubt occurring about each of them, because it is possible she intended zina before marriage, in which case hadd would be obligatory without liʿān, because she confirmed him and he did not confirm her; and it is possible she intended 'my zina was with you after marriage, because I did not allow anyone else' and this is the intended meaning in such a situation. On this consideration, liʿān would be obligatory without hadd upon the woman, because qadhf exists from him and not from her. So what we said stands. Translation: And if the wife said in response to her husband's (yā zāniya): 'Yes, I committed zina with you,' then hadd is not obligatory, nor is liʿān obligatory. That is, the husband said to his wife 'yā zāniya' and she said in reply 'I committed zina with you.' Then nothing is obligatory from the two punishments. Because doubt has arisen about each of them. Because it is possible the woman meant 'I committed zina with you before marriage' – in that case hadd would be obligatory and not liʿān, because the woman confirmed the husband's statement but there is no confirmation from his side. And it is possible she meant 'my zina was with you after marriage, because I did not allow anyone else' and that is the intended meaning in such a situation. On this consideration, liʿān would be obligatory without the hadd on the woman, because qadhf exists from him and not from her. So what we said stands.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage examines another scenario in husband-wife mutual accusations. If the wife, after being called 'yā zāniya', responds with 'zanaytu bika' (I committed zina with you), the ruling is that neither hadd nor liʿān is imposed. The reason is doubt (shakk) about the meaning of her statement. She could be admitting to zina before marriage, which would confirm the husband's accusation and thus make her liable for hadd (since she confessed), but the husband would not do liʿān because she confirmed him. Alternatively, she could mean that she never committed zina except with him after marriage (i.e., she is chaste), which would make the husband's accusation false and require liʿān from him. Because both interpretations are possible, the doubt prevents the application of either punishment. The principle is that punishments are suspended when there is uncertainty. This ruling safeguards against imposing a harsh punishment in ambiguous circumstances.

Key Terms
شک (shakk): Doubt or uncertainty that prevents punishment; a principle in Islamic criminal law.
حد (ḥadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by Islamic law for certain crimes, such as zina.
لعان (liʿān): A mutual oath-taking procedure between spouses when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband calls his wife 'yā zāniya' and she replies 'zanaytu bika', what is the Hanafī ruling?
A) The wife receives hadd and the husband does liʿān.
B) The husband receives hadd and the wife does liʿān.
C) Neither hadd nor liʿān is imposed.
D) Both hadd and liʿān are imposed.

Key Takeaway
Doubt (shubha) in a confession or accusation prevents the application of ḥadd and liʿān in Hanafī fiqh.

Doubt (Shakk) Suspends Hadd: Wife Says 'Zanaytu Bika'
Explains ruling when wife responds 'zanaytu bika' to husband's accusation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of doubt (shakk) to determine that neither hadd nor li'an is imposed when a wife says 'I committed zina with you' in response to an accusation, and explain the two possible interpretations that create the doubt.

Core Idea
Doubt (shakk) drops hadd: a wife's ambiguous reply can mean either zina before marriage or marital intimacy.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, al-Marghinani examines a specific scenario in the law of hudud and li'an. When a husband accuses his wife of zina and she replies 'I committed zina with you,' the Hanafi school holds that neither the hadd punishment for slander (qadhf) nor li'an is imposed. This is not because her statement is ignored, but because it is ambiguous. By understanding the two possible meanings, you will see how the principle of doubt (shakk) operates to suspend fixed punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the initial ruling
The husband says 'O adulteress' to his wife; she replies 'I committed zina with you.' The text states: no hadd, no li'an.
This is the baseline ruling from the matn.
2
Recall the general rule: doubt suspends hadd
In Hanafi fiqh, if there is any plausible doubt (shakk) in the application of a hadd punishment, it is not carried out. Here, the wife's statement introduces ambiguity.
Principle: al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat.
3
Separate the two possible interpretations
Interpretation 1: She refers to zina before marriage, thus confirming his accusation. Interpretation 2: She refers to marital intercourse, thus denying his accusation.
The key is the phrase 'bika' which can mean 'with you' in either context.
4
Trace the legal consequences of each interpretation
If interpretation 1 (pre-marital): she admits to zina → hadd on her; his accusation is true → no qadhf, no li'an. If interpretation 2 (marital only): husband falsely accused → he is liable for qadhf, and li'an is required to avoid hadd. But the text says under interpretation 2, li'an is obligatory without hadd on the woman.
The commentator confirms that under the second interpretation, li'an is on him.
5
Apply the doubt principle: neither interpretation can be chosen
Since we cannot determine which meaning she intended, both possibilities are equally valid. Doubt prevails, and both punishments (hadd and li'an) are suspended.
The result: no hadd and no li'an, as stated in the matn.
6
State the mufta bihi ruling
The operative ruling for fatwa: no hadd and no li'an when the wife says 'zanaytu bika' in response to an accusation.
This is part of zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَتْ زَنَيْتُ بِكَ فَلَا حَدَّوَ لَا لِعَانَ وَمَعْنَاهُ قَالَتْ بَعْدَ مَا قَالَ لَهَا يَا زَانِيَةُ لِوُقُوعِ الشَّكِ فِي كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا لأَنَّهُ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنَّهَا اَرَادَتِ الزِّنَاءَ قَبْلَ النِّكَاحِ فَيَجِبُ الْحَدُّ دُونَ اللَّعَانِ لِتَصْدِ يُقِهَا إِيَّاهُ وَإِنْعِدَامِهِ مِنْهُ وَيَحْتَمِلُهُ انَّهَا أَرَادَتْ زِنَانِي مَا كَانَ مَعَكَ بَعْدَ النِكَاح لانّى مَا مَكَنْتُ أَحَدًا غَيْرَكَ وَهُوَ الْمُرَادُ فِي مِثْلِ هَذِهِ الْحَالَةِ وَعَلَى هذَا الْإِعْتِبَارِ يَجِبُ العَانُ دُونَ الْحَةِ عَلَى الْمَرْأَةِ لِوُجُودِ الْقَدْفِ مِنْهُ وَعَدْمِهِ مِنْهَا فَجَاءَ مَا قُلْنَا ترجمہ اور اگر بیوی نے شوہر کے ( اوزانیہ کے ) جواب میں کہا کہ ہاں میں نے تمہارے ساتھ ہی تو زنا کیا ہے۔ تو حد واجب نہ ہوگی اور نہ لعان واجب ہوگا۔ یعنی شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا یا زانیہ تو اس نے اس کے جواب میں کہا میں نے تمہارے ساتھ زنا کیا ہے۔ تو صداجان میں سے کچھ بھی واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ دونوں میں سے ہر ایک میں شک پیدا ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ شاید عورت کی مراد یہ ہو کہ میں نے نکاح سے پہلے تم سے زنا کیا ہے۔ تو ایسا ۔ ہونے سے حد واجب ہوگی اور لعان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عورت نے شوہر کے قول کی تصدیق کی مگر شوہر کی طرف سے اسکی تصدیق نہیں پائی گئی اور شاید عورت کی یہ مراد ہو کہ میر از نا تو وہی ولی ہے جو تمہارے ساتھ نکاح کے بعد ہوئی ہے کیونکہ میں نے تمہارے علاوہ کسی دوسرے مرد کو خود پر قدرت جماع نہیں دی ہے اور ایسی حالت میں یہی مراد ہوتی ہے اور اس اعتبار پر لعان واجب ہوگا اور عورت پر حد قذف واجب نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ شوہر کی طرف سے زنا کا بہتان پایا گیا ہے۔ لیکن بیوی کی طرف سے نہیں پایا گیا ہے اور جب دونوں احتمال ہیں تو نتیجہ وہی نکلا جو پہلے بیان کر دیا ہے کہ شرک کی وجہ سے نہ حد قذف واجب ہوگی اور نہ لعان واجب ہوگا۔
And if she said 'I committed zina with you,' then there is no hadd and no li'an. Its meaning: she said after he had said to her 'O adulteress,' due to the occurrence of doubt in each of the two, because it is possible that she meant zina before marriage; then hadd is obligatory without li'an because she confirmed him, and he did not [confirm her own accusation]. And it is possible that she meant 'my zina that was with you after marriage, because I did not allow anyone other than you,' and that is what is intended in such a situation. On this consideration, li'an is obligatory without hadd on the woman, because the slander existed from him and not from her. So what we said applies. Translation: And if the wife said in response to the husband's (o adulteress) that yes, I have committed zina with you. Then no hadd is obligatory and no li'an is obligatory. Meaning the husband said to his wife 'O adulteress,' and she replied, 'I committed zina with you.' Then nothing from the two [hadd or li'an] is obligatory. Because doubt occurred in each of the two. Because perhaps the woman meant that I committed zina with you before marriage. If so, then hadd becomes obligatory and li'an is not obligatory, because the woman confirmed the husband's statement, but from the husband's side there was no confirmation of her statement. And perhaps the woman meant that my zina is that which was with you after marriage, because I have not allowed any other man to have power of intercourse over me, and in such a state, this is what is intended. On this consideration, li'an is obligatory and hadd for slander is not obligatory on the woman, because the accusation of zina came from the husband, not from her. And when both probabilities exist, the result is as we stated: due to doubt, neither hadd nor li'an is obligatory.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a scenario where, after the husband calls his wife 'O adulteress,' she responds with 'I committed zina with you.' The text explains that due to the possibility of two meanings, doubt (shakk) arises, and as a result neither hadd (slander punishment) nor li'an is imposed. The two possible meanings: (1) She may mean she committed zina before marriage, in which case she admits a false accusation? Actually she confirms the husband's accusation of her being an adulteress, so hadd would be on her (as she admits to zina? But the text says hadd is obligatory without li'an because she confirmed him, but the husband did not confirm her counter-accusation. This seems to imply that if she means pre-marital zina, she is not slandering the husband, so no li'an. (2) She may mean that her zina with him occurred only within marriage (i.e., she is not an adulteress because she only had intercourse with him), effectively saying the husband is lying. In that case, li'an is obligatory because the husband slandered her, and she did not slander him. Since both interpretations are possible, the ruling suspends both hadd and li'an due to the doubt.

Key Terms
شك (shakk): Doubt or uncertainty regarding the meaning of a statement, which leads to suspending the hadd punishment.
احتمال (ihtimal): Possibility or probability of meaning; here, two possible interpretations of the wife's words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi madhhab rule that neither hadd nor li'an is imposed when a wife says 'I committed zina with you' in response to her husband's accusation?
A) Because the wife's statement is considered a lie.
B) Because her statement can be interpreted in two ways (pre-marital zina or marital intercourse), causing doubt.
C) Because the husband's accusation is stronger than the wife's reply.
D) Because the wife automatically forgoes her right to li'an.

Key Takeaway
When a wife responds 'zanaytu bika,' the Hanafi rule of doubt (shakk) suspends both hadd and li'an because her words can mean either pre-marital zina (confirming) or marital intimacy (denying).

Acknowledgment and Denial of Paternity: Li'an or Hadd?
Ruling with rational proof on li'an vs hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two scenarios of iqrar and nafy and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of paternity is binding; denial after it is slander requiring li'an, but denial then acknowledgment triggers hadd.

This passage from Al-Hidayah examines a crucial question: what happens when a husband first acknowledges a child as his own, then later denies paternity? Or denies first, then acknowledges? The ruling depends on the order of actions, teaching us the binding nature of iqrar and the role of li'an as a tool to resolve mutual accusation. Understanding this distinction helps in grasping the function of li'an and the importance of consistency in statements regarding lineage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two opposing sequences: (1) husband acknowledges then denies, (2) husband denies then acknowledges. Note the order and the resulting ruling for each.
2
Analyze the effect of initial acknowledgment
In scenario 1, the acknowledgment (iqrar) makes the lineage (nasab) binding. What legal consequence does this create for the subsequent denial?
The denial after binding iqrar constitutes slander (qadhf) against the wife, because the child is already legally his.
3
Understand the necessity of li'an
Once he becomes a slanderer, li'an (mutual imprecation) becomes wajib. Why is li'an the remedy here rather than immediate hadd?
Li'an is prescribed to resolve mutual accusation without witnesses; it is a necessity (darurah) for the couple.
4
Examine the reverse scenario
In scenario 2, the husband denies first, which would normally lead to li'an. But when he later acknowledges, his acknowledgment belies his previous denial. What happens to the li'an and what replaces it?
His acknowledgment makes the mutual accusation cease, so li'an is invalidated. The original rule (asl) of hadd for slander applies because his denial was an accusation of adultery against his wife.
5
Confirm the child's lineage
In both scenarios, the child remains his, because he acknowledged paternity at some point. Li'an does not require cutting off lineage to be valid.
Li'an can be performed even without a child (e.g., mere accusation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ أَقَرَّ بِوَلَدٍ ثُمَّ نَفَاهُ فَإِنَّهُ يُلاعِنُ لأنَّ النَّسْبَ لَزِمَهُ بِإِقْرَارِهِ وَبَالنَّفْيِ بَعْدَهُ صَارَقَا ذِ فَا فَيُلَاعِنُ وَإِنْ نَفَاهُ ثُمَّ أَقَرَّبِهِ حدَّلَا نَّهُ لَمَّا أَكْذَبَ نَفْسَهُ بَطَلَ اللَّعَادُ لِأَنَّهُ جَدَّ ضَرُورِيٌّ صُرَالِيْهِ ضُرُورَةَ التَّكَاذُبِ وَالْأَصْلُ فِيْهِ حَدُّ الْقَذْفِ فَإِذَا بَطَلَ التَّكَاذُبُ يُصَارُ إِلَى الْأَصْلِ وَالْوَلَدُ وَلَدُهُ فِي الْوَجْهَيْنِ لِإِقْرَارِهِ بِهِ سَابَقًا أَوْلَاحِقاوَ اللَّعَانُ يَصِحُ بِدُون قَطْعِ النَّشْبِ كَمَا يَضِحُ بِدُونِ الْوَلَدِ
ترجمہ اور اگر شوہر نے پہلے تو اپنے لڑکے کے نسب کا اقرار کیا بعد میں اس کی نفی کر دی تو اس پر لعان واجب ہوگا۔ کیونکہ پہلے اس کے اقرار سے نسب لازم ہو گیا۔ پھر جب نسب کی نفی کی تو اس طرح بیوی پر زنا کی تہمت لگانے والا ہو گیا اس لئے لعان کرے گا اور اگر شوہر نے پہلے بچہ کے نسب کا انکار کیا بعد میں اقرار کر لیا کہ یہ بچہ میرا ہی ہے۔ تو شوہر کو حد قذف لگائی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ جب اس نے خود کو جھوٹا ہونا بان کیا تو لعان باطل ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ ایک مجبوری کی بناء پر لعان کا حکم دیا جاتا ہے۔ کیونکہ میاں اور بیوی دونوں ہی ایک دوسرے کو جھٹلاتے ہیں۔ حالانکہ ان کے پاس ایک بھی گواہ نہیں ہے۔ ایسی صورت میں ضرور لحان لیا جائے گا اور اس میں اصل حکم حد قذف کا واجب ہونا ہے اور شوہر نے خود کو جھٹلا کر دونوں طرف کے اختلاف کو ختم کر دیا تو اب اصل حکم یعنی حد قذف پر عمل کرنا ہوگا اور دونوں صورتوں میں ہی وہ بچہ اسی مرد کا ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے بچہ کا اقرار کر لیا ہے خواہ
پہلے کیا ہو یا بعد میں کیا ہو۔ اور قطع نسب کے بغیر بھی لعان کا واقع ہونا صحیح ہے۔ جیسے کہ لڑکے کے بغیر بھی صحیح ہے۔
And whoever acknowledges a child then denies it, then he performs li'an because the lineage becomes binding by his acknowledgment, and by the denial after that he becomes a slanderer, so he performs li'an. And if he denies it then acknowledges it, then hadd [of slander] is upon him, because when he belied himself, li'an is invalidated because it was necessitated [only] for the need of the mutual denial, and the principle in it is the slander punishment. So when the denial is invalidated, one reverts to the principle. And the child is his child in both cases due to his acknowledgment, whether earlier or later. And li'an is valid without cutting off lineage just as it is valid without a child. Translation: And if the husband first acknowledged the lineage of his son, then later denied it, then li'an is obligatory upon him. Because first by his acknowledgment the lineage became binding. Then when he denied the lineage, he thus became an accuser of his wife of adultery, so he will perform li'an. And if the husband first denied the lineage of the child, then later acknowledged that this child is indeed mine, then the slander punishment will be inflicted upon him. Because when he belied himself, li'an became invalid. Because li'an is prescribed only out of necessity, due to the mutual denial of husband and wife despite having no witnesses. In such a case, li'an is resorted to. And the original ruling in it is the obligation of the slander punishment. And when the husband belied himself, he eliminated the difference [mutual denial], so now the original ruling (hadd for slander) is applied. And in both cases, that child is the man's child. Because he acknowledged the child, whether earlier or later. And li'an is valid without cutting off lineage, just as it is valid without a child.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding two scenarios involving acknowledgment (iqrar) and denial (nafy) of a child's paternity. First scenario: The husband acknowledges the child, then later denies it. His initial acknowledgment fixed the lineage (nasab), so the subsequent denial implies he is accusing his wife of adultery (since the child is already attributed to him). Therefore, li'an becomes wajib. Second scenario: He denies the child first, then later acknowledges it. In this case, after denial, li'an might have been initiated but becomes invalid because he has now confirmed the child. The principle is that li'an is a necessity to resolve mutual accusation between spouses without witnesses. When the husband acknowledges, the mutual accusation ceases, and the original ruling of hadd for slander (since he accused his wife of adultery by denying paternity) applies. In both scenarios, the child's lineage is established with the husband because of his acknowledgment, whether initial or subsequent. Also, li'an can be performed even if there is no child (e.g., when merely accusing of adultery).

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of paternity; it establishes the child's lineage (nasab) conclusively.
نفی (nafy): Denial of paternity; after acknowledgment, it amounts to slander (qadhf) of the wife.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternal affiliation; it is established by marriage, acknowledgment, or clear proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband first denies a child and then later acknowledges him as his own, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Li'an is performed because the child's lineage is doubtful.
B) The husband must pay a fine.
C) The husband is subjected to the slander punishment (hadd) because he belied himself, invalidating li'an.
D) Both hadd and li'an are applied.

Key Takeaway
The order of acknowledgment and denial determines the remedy: acknowledgment then denial → li'an; denial then acknowledgment → hadd.

When Hadd al-Qadhf Drops: Signs of Zina in the Accused
Ruling on when hadd is dropped for accusing a woman with signs of zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the condition of ihsan is absent and state that hadd al-qadhf is dropped.

Core Idea
Hadd al-qadhf requires the accused to be chaste; visible signs of zina remove chastity and thus hadd.

The hadd punishment for false accusation of zina is severe, but it only applies when the accused is chaste (muhsan). This lesson explores a key exception: when a woman already has apparent signs of zina—such as children with no known father or having undergone li'an with a living child—the accuser is not punished. Understanding this condition helps you see how Islamic law balances honour protection with realistic social contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling for qadhf
Qadhf (accusing a chaste person of zina) normally carries hadd (80 lashes) if the accuser cannot produce four witnesses. Recognize that this is the general rule.
2
Locate the condition for hadd
The hadd only applies if the accused is muhsan (chaste). This condition is central to the Hanafi ruling. Find the term 'ihsan' in the passage.
Ihsan means being free from any suspicion of zina.
3
Identify signs that negate ihsan
The passage lists two signs: children with no known father, and having performed li'an (mutual cursing) with a living child. These establish 'amarat al-zina' (sign of zina).
The existence of a child without a father is considered a clear sign in society.
4
Apply the exception
When such signs exist, the woman is not considered muhsan. Therefore, anyone who accuses her of zina is not liable for hadd. The ruling is 'no hadd'.
5
Contrast with absence of signs
If the woman performed li'an without a child, there is no external sign of zina. She remains muhsan, so the accuser must receive hadd.
Li'an alone, without a child, does not create a sign visible to third parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ قَذَفَ اِمْرَأَةٌ وَ مَعَهَا أَوْلَادٌ لَا يُعْرَفُ لَهُمْ اَبِّ أَوْ قَذَفَ الْمُلَاعِنَةَ بِوَلَدٍ وَالْوَلَدَحَيٌّ أَوْقَذَفَهَا بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْوَلَدِ فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ لِقِيَامِ آمَارَةِ الزِّنَاءِ مِنْهَا وَهِيَ وِلَادَةُ وَلَدِلَا آبَ لَهُ فَفَاتَتِ الْعِفَّةُ نَظُرًا إِلَيْهَا وَهِيَ شَرْطُ الْإِحْصَانِ وَلَوْ قَذَفَ اِمْرَأَةٌ لَا عَنَتْ بِغَيْرِ وَلَدٍ فَعَلَيْهِ الْحَدُّ لِعَدَمِ آمَارَةِ الزَّنَاءِ
And whoever accuses a woman and with her are children whose father is not known, or accuses a woman who performed li'an with a child while the child is alive, or accuses her after the death of the child, then no hadd is upon him because the sign of zina is established from her, which is giving birth to a child with no father. So chastity is lost in her regard, and it is a condition of ihsan. And if he accuses a woman who performed li'an without a child, then upon him is hadd because of the absence of a sign of zina.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage discusses situations where a woman already has apparent signs of zina: either she has children with no known father, or she has previously undergone li'an with a living or deceased child. In these cases, her reputation for chastity ('iffah) is already compromised, so the condition for ihsan (being muhsan) necessary for hadd al-qadhf is not met. Therefore, anyone who accuses her of zina is not subject to hadd. However, if a woman performed li'an without any child (i.e., she accused her husband of impotence or something else without a child), then no external sign of zina is present, so her chastity remains intact, and the accuser is liable for hadd al-qadhf.

Key Terms
إِحْصَان (ihsan): The quality of being chaste (free from suspicion of zina). A condition for hadd al-qadhf.
أَمَارَةُ الزِّنَاء (amarat al-zina): A sign or indication of zina, such as a child without a known father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the accused is a woman with no known father for her children.
B) When the accused is a man.
C) When the accusation is made privately.
D) When the accuser is the husband.

Key Takeaway
Hadd al-qadhf is dropped when the accused woman has an apparent sign of zina, such as children without a known father or having performed li'an with a child, because such signs negate the condition of ihsan.

Qadhf of a Man Who Committed Zina in Ghair Milk
Hadd not imposed on one who accuses a man of zina when the man is not muhsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inherently prohibited intercourse (zina) and temporarily prohibited intercourse, and state when a qadhf accuser avoids hadd.

Core Idea
A person is not muhsan if he has engaged in inherently prohibited intercourse; an accuser of such a person is truthful and avoids hadd.

This passage from al-Hidayah explains a critical principle in the law of qadhf (false accusation of zina). The key is the concept of ihsan (chastity). If the person accused has committed zina (inherently prohibited intercourse), then he is not muhsan, and the accuser is speaking truth, so no hadd. But what counts as zina? The Hanafi school distinguishes between intercourse that is forbidden in itself (haram li-aynihi) and intercourse forbidden due to a temporary circumstance (haram li-ghayrihi). Only the former makes a person non-muhsan. Abu Hanifa adds an additional condition: the prohibition must be permanent and established by consensus or a famous hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The accused has engaged in intercourse outside his ownership (ghair milk). The accuser is not subjected to hadd.
The ruling hinges on the nature of the intercourse.
2
Extract the key principle
The ruling depends on whether the intercourse is haram li-aynihi (inherently forbidden, i.e., zina) or haram li-ghayrihi (forbidden due to external circumstance).
Only the former destroys ihsan.
3
Define ihsan and its loss
Ihsan (chastity) is lost when a person commits zina. Only inherently forbidden intercourse counts as zina.
Temporary prohibitions do not count.
4
Apply Abu Hanifa's condition
The permanent prohibition must be established by ijma' or a famous hadith. This ensures certainty and avoids ambiguity.
This condition is unique to Abu Hanifa.
5
Test the boundaries
Consider intercourse with a woman in her menses (temporal prohibition). It is haram li-ghayrihi, so it is not zina, and the person remains muhsan.
The accuser would be subject to hadd if he cannot prove it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ وَطِيَ وَطُيَا حَرَامَ فِي غَيْرِ مِلْكِهِ لَمْ يُحَدُّ قَادِقُهُ لِفَوَاتِ الْعِفَّةِ وَهِيَ شَرْطُ الْإِحْصَانِ وَلَانَّ الْقَاذِفَ صَادِق وَالْأَصْلُ فِيهِ أَنَّ مَنْ وَطِيَ وَطَيَا حَرَامًا لِعَيْنِهِ لَا يَجِبُ الْحَدُّ بِقَلْفِهِ لاَنَّ الزِّنَا هُوَ الْوَطْئُ الْمُحَرَّمُ لِعَيْنِهِ وَإِنْ كَانَ مُحَرَّمًا لِغَيْرِهِ يُحَدُّ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِزِنَا فَالْوَطْئُ فِي غَيْرِ الْمِلْكِ مِنْ كُلَّ وَجِّه أَوْ مِنْ وَجْهِ حَرَامٌ لِعَيْنِهِ وَكَدَ الْوَطَأُ فِي الْمِلْكِ وَالْحُرْمَةُ مُؤيَّدَةٌ فَإِنْ كَانَتِ الْحُرْمَةُ مُوَقَتَةً فَالْحُرْمَةُ لِغَيْرِهِ وَأَبُو حَنِيفَةٌ يَشْتَرِطُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْحُرْمَةُ الْمَوْيَّدَةُ ثَابِتَةٌ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ اَوْ بِالْحَدِيْثِ الْمَشْهُورِ لِتَكُونَ ثَابِتَةٌ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَرَدُّ دِبَيَانُهُ أَنَّ مَنْ قَذَفَ رَجُلًا
He said: Whoever has intercourse that is forbidden outside his ownership, his accuser is not subject to hadd because of the loss of chastity, and it is a condition of ihsan, and because the accuser is truthful. And the principle in this is that whoever has intercourse that is forbidden in itself (li-aynihi), hadd is not obligatory upon his accuser, because zina is intercourse forbidden in itself. But if it is forbidden due to something else (li-ghayrihi), then hadd is imposed because it is not zina. So intercourse outside ownership, whether in every respect or in some respect, is forbidden in itself. Similarly, intercourse in ownership [with permanent prohibition]... and if the prohibition is temporary, then the prohibition is due to something else. And Abu Hanifa stipulates that the permanent prohibition must be established by consensus or known hadith to be established without hesitation. Its explanation: whoever accuses a man...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle: for hadd al-qadhf to apply, the accused person must be muhsan (chaste). If the person accused has engaged in zina (permanently prohibited intercourse), then he is not muhsan, and the accuser is deemed truthful, so no hadd. The text distinguishes between 'haram li-aynihi' (inherently forbidden, i.e., zina) and 'haram li-ghayrihi' (forbidden due to external circumstance, e.g., intercourse during menses or with a woman in iddah). Only the former is considered zina. Abu Hanifa adds a condition: the permanent prohibition must be agreed upon by consensus (ijma') or a famous hadith (mashhur) to be clearly established. This is because there is some variance in what counts as 'zina' across different contexts.

Key Terms
وَطْءٌ حَرَامٌ لِعَيْنِهِ (wat'un haramun li-aynihi): Intercourse that is inherently prohibited (zina) without any temporary excuse.
إِحْصَان (ihsan): Chastity; being free from any known act of zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the accused engaged in intercourse prohibited by a permanent prohibition established by consensus or famous hadith.
C) Always when the accused is male.
D) Never; hadd is always imposed.

Key Takeaway
A qadhf accuser avoids hadd only if the accused has committed inherently prohibited intercourse (zina) established by a permanent prohibition.

The Legal Effect of Denying Paternity: No Hadd or Li'an
Hanafi ruling on paternity denial wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband denies both his and his wife's parenthood, and distinguish it from a simple denial of paternity that triggers li'an.

Core Idea
A husband who denies the child is his or his wife's avoids hadd and li'an by denying the birth itself.

What happens when a husband sees a child and declares it is neither his nor his wife's? This short but precise passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies that such a statement does not constitute qadhf (slander) because it does not accuse the wife of zinā; rather, it negates the child's existence. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the hadd penalties and the li'an procedure.

Guided Path
1
Read the exact phrasing
Identify the precise words used by the husband: 'This is not my son nor your son.' Note that he denies both parents separately.
The Arabic uses 'ibnī' (my son) and 'ibniki' (your son) to refer to the wife.
2
Identify the legal category of the statement
Classify the statement: is it an explicit accusation of zinā? The passage says it is a denial of the birth (inkār al-wilādah), not qadhf.
Qadhf requires attributing zinā to a specific person. Here, there is no such attribution.
3
Conclude the absence of hadd and li'an
Since the statement is not qadhf, the fixed penalty (hadd) for false accusation does not apply. Also, since the husband does not accuse his wife of zinā, there is no need for li'an.
Li'an is specifically for a husband who accuses his wife of zinā without witnesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ لَيْسَ بِابْنِى وَ لَا بِابْنِكَ فَلَاحَدٌ وَ لَا لِعَانَ لأَنَّهُ انْكَرَ الْوِلَادَةَ وَ بِهِ لَا يَصِيرُ قَاذِفًا
And if he said, 'This is not my son nor your son,' then there is no hadd and no li'an, because he denied the birth, and by that he does not become a slanderer.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the chapter on hadd al-qadhf (punishment for slander). The passage addresses a scenario where a husband, upon seeing a child, denies being the father and also denies that his wife is the mother. By denying both, he is not accusing his wife of adultery; rather, he is denying the very birth of the child. Therefore, neither the fixed punishment for slander (hadd al-qadhf) nor the procedure of li'an (mutual imprecation) are triggered. The key legal point is that for hadd to apply, the slanderer must explicitly accuse a specific person of zinā. Here, the husband's words are construed as a denial of the child's existence, not an accusation against his wife.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): The specific fixed corporal punishment for certain crimes, here for false accusation of zinā (qadhf).
لعان (li'an): The procedure of mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of zinā without witnesses.
قذف (qadhf): The act of accusing a chaste person of zinā, which incurs the hadd penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must perform li'an.
B) He is liable for hadd al-qadhf.
C) No hadd and no li'an.
D) Hadd is waived but li'an is required.

Key Takeaway
A husband's denial of paternity does not automatically trigger hadd or li'an; the exact wording must be examined to see if it constitutes an accusation of zinā or a denial of birth.

Slandering Women with Apparent Signs of Zinā: When Hadd is Dropped
Ruling on slandering women with children of unknown father or who performed li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the hadd punishment for slander (hadd al-qadhf) is not applied, and explain the role of circumstantial evidence (amārah) in determining chastity.

Core Idea
Visible signs of zinā negate the presumption of chastity, so slandering such a woman carries no hadd.

The hadd punishment for false accusation of zinā is severe, but it is not absolute. The law requires the victim to be muhsan – meaning free, adult, Muslim, and chaste. This lesson examines a key exception: when the slandered woman displays clear indications of zinā, such as having a child with no known father, her presumed chastity is lost, and the slanderer escapes the fixed punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline ruling
Slandering a chaste woman (hadd al-qadhf) normally incurs 80 lashes. The victim must be muhsan: free, adult, Muslim, and chaste.
2
Recognize the exceptional cases
The text lists three scenarios: a woman with children of unknown father, a mulā'inah with a child (alive or dead). All involve a child as a sign.
The child need not be alive; even after death the indication persists.
3
Extract the underlying cause ('illah)
The common factor is the existence of a child without a recognized father. This is an amārah (circumstantial evidence) of zinā.
The text explicitly states 'because there exists an indication of zinā'.
4
Connect to the condition of ihsan
Chastity ('iffah) is a condition for ihsan. The amārah destroys the appearance of 'iffah, so the victim is not muhsan, and hadd drops.
The text says 'so chastity is lost in her regard, and it is a condition of ihsan'.
5
Apply the contrast
If the mulā'inah has no child, there is no amārah, so 'iffah remains, and hadd applies. This confirms the decisive role of the child.
The text explicitly states the opposite ruling: hadd is imposed when there is no child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ قَذَفَ اِمْرَأَةً وَ مَعَهَا أَوْلَادٌ لَا يُعْرَفُ لَهُمْ أَبٌ أَوْ قَذَفَ الْمُلَاعِنَةَ بِوَلَدٍ وَالْوَلَدُ حَيٌّ أَوْ قَذَفَهَا بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْوَلَدِ فَلَا حَدَّ عَلَيْهِ لِقِيَامِ أَمَارَةِ الزِّنَاءِ مِنْهَا وَهِيَ وِلَادَةُ وَلَدٍ لَا أَبَ لَهُ فَفَاتَتِ الْعِفَّةُ نَظَرًا إِلَيْهَا وَهِيَ شَرْطُ الْإِحْصَانِ وَلَوْ قَذَفَ اِمْرَأَةً لَاعَنَتْ بِغَيْرِ وَلَدٍ فَعَلَيْهِ الْحَدُّ لِانْعِدَامِ أَمَارَةِ الزِّنَاءِ
And whoever slanders a woman who has children whose father is unknown, or slanders a woman who performed li'an regarding a child while the child is alive, or slanders her after the child's death, then there is no hadd upon him because there exists an indication of zinā from her, which is the birth of a child without a father, so chastity ('iffah) is lost in her regard, and it is a condition of ihsan. And if he slanders a woman who performed li'an without a child, then hadd is upon him due to the absence of an indication of zinā.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with its proof from Al-Hidayah. The ruling: anyone who accuses a woman of zinā when visible signs of zinā exist – such as children with no known father (typically from a previous adulterous relationship) or a woman who had already performed li'an regarding a child (whether the child is alive or dead) – incurs no hadd punishment. The reason (talil) is that these cases provide circumstantial evidence (amārah) of zinā, which destroys the woman's apparent chastity ('iffah). Since 'iffah is a condition for ihsan (the state that makes slander punishable by hadd), the absence of 'iffah removes the hadd. Conversely, if a woman performed li'an without having a child from that situation, there is no such sign; hence her 'iffah remains presumed, and the slanderer faces hadd. The underlying principle is that hadd al-qadhf requires the victim to be muhsan: free, adult, Muslim, and chaste. Visible signs of zinā negate the presumption of chastity.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): The state of being free, adult, Muslim, and chaste (not having committed zinā). For the purpose of hadd al-qadhf, these conditions must be present in the slandered person.
عفة ('iffah): Chastity; freedom from actual or apparent zinā. In this context, it is a condition for ihsan: if 'iffah is lost due to apparent zinā, then slander is not punishable by hadd.
أمارة (amārah): An indication or circumstantial evidence that points to a fact. Here, the child without a father serves as an amārah of zinā.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman has a child from a previous relationship without a known father. Someone calls her a zāniyah. What is the ruling on the slanderer?
A) He must receive 80 lashes.
B) No hadd because she lacks 'iffah.
C) He must perform li'an.
D) He is given a discretionary punishment.

Key Takeaway
When a woman has a child with no known father or has performed li'an regarding a child, her apparent chastity is negated, and slandering her does not incur hadd.

Haram Intercourse and Its Effect on Slander Punishment
Legal principle distinguishing inherently prohibited intercourse from externally prohibited intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inherently and externally prohibited intercourse and apply the Hanafi rule that only inherently prohibited intercourse removes 'iffah, thus exempting the slanderer from hadd.

Core Idea
Only inherently forbidden intercourse makes a person a zānī; externally forbidden intercourse does not, so slander incurs hadd.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies a critical nuance in Hanafi criminal law: not all prohibited sexual acts are considered zinā for the purpose of slander punishment. The author explains that the legal effect on 'iffah (chastity) depends on whether the prohibition is intrinsic to the act or arises from external circumstances. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the hadd of qadhf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The passage begins with a ruling: if someone commits forbidden intercourse outside his ownership, his slanderer is not punished with hadd. Note the condition 'outside his ownership' – this signals the key concept of milk.
The ruling is about the slanderer, not the person committing the act.
2
Understand the legal criterion: haram li-'aynihi vs. li-ghayrihi
The author states the principle: if the intercourse is inherently forbidden (li-'aynihi), the slanderer is not punished because the act is zinā, and the person has lost 'iffah. If forbidden due to an external factor (li-ghayrihi), the act is not zinā, and 'iffah is preserved, so slander incurs hadd. Classify the given examples according to this dichotomy.
Examples: outright fornication = li-'aynihi; intercourse during menses = li-ghayrihi.
3
Apply the criterion to ownership scenarios
The passage explains that intercourse outside full ownership (partial or no milk) is inherently forbidden. Similarly, intercourse within ownership but with a permanent prohibition (e.g., with a mahram) is also inherently forbidden. Temporary prohibitions (e.g., during fasting) are externally forbidden. Use the jointly-owned slave-girl example to test your understanding.
Complete milk = ownership in all respects; partial milk = joint ownership.
4
Note Imam Abu Hanifa's additional condition
Abu Hanifa requires that the permanent prohibition be established by ijmā' or a mashhūr hadith to ensure certainty. This is a condition for applying the 'inherently forbidden' classification. Consider how this affects the ruling.
This condition is unique to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn may not require it.
5
Synthesize and apply to new scenarios
Now, apply the full framework: given a scenario, determine whether the intercourse is haram li-'aynihi or li-ghayrihi, then state the ruling on the slanderer (hadd or no hadd).
Practice with scenarios from different fiqh contexts (e.g., marriage, slavery, iḥrām).
